Chapter 5

The road to the moment of discovery is a difficult one; it leaves you
wondering how you got there, where you’re going, and where you’ve
been.  It’s one of those things that will lead you to a goal but will
certainly obscure your perception.  But when you reach the peace of
knowing you’ve made it through sheer hell, the taste of your victory
is sweet.  You know you’ve endured the worst life could dish out. 
You’ve tolerated and fought and survived; the peace and unshakable
knowledge you acquire is your reward.

I had been on the road for a long time, I was at the edge of a
precipice trying to decide whether or not I should throw myself off, not
knowing if there were a ledge ten feet down or jagged rocks hundreds of
feet below.  I was standing in the light of my own bitterness; the
debauched life that was promised had not shown itself.  Gale was
seemingly living it every time I was away.  But I wasn’t.  I was
angry, resentful, and perhaps jealous.  I immersed myself in hatred. 
Down below, in the darkness beyond my footing, could lie a better
outlook, a better life, but I could also fall to my death not ever
knowing if I had succeeded or not.  A sacrifice had to be made.  I had
to take a leap of faith.

Gale gave me a tarot reading.  I asked her not to, but she said,

“Indulge me, I need the practice.”

She had gotten the idea to give professional readings in the apartment

something and I didn’t want to see how fucked my perception was.  She
made a good point, but I had the feeling I would only learn what I
already knew.

She set out the cards and turned them over one by one, relating the
meanings as she went along.

“You’re getting a lot of aces and reverses.”

“You have to let go of some of the shit that’s going on now and
throw yourself into the belief that you need to live a new life.  You
have many conflicts to resolve.”

The rush of air lifted my heart; I fell and fell.  The light dissipated

all I knew was anxiety, fear of falling, and fear of striking the
ground.  I had fallen so many times recently and was pretty damn tired

could have been easily confused for the passing air.

“Stay if you want, go if you must, I understand your confusion.  For

A radio played in the darkness.  I tried to move.  The wind had stopped;
my fall was broken.  I reached out toward the music; the volume was
turned up.  Light came from an unknown source, and then I saw her by the

appeared as a ghost, perfect.  Her long hair flowed to her long dress;
she smiled.  I felt her touch—the light became brighter until my sight
was a wash of white.

I felt free, released, away from my body.  I was floating aimlessly.  I

inanimate pie on the plate before me, she was gelatin, congealed—soft
but firm.  I never liked gelatin.

We sat in the living room; she was close to him, he didn’t seem to
mind.  As time moved on and the conversation dragged, she began
caressing him.  They started talking to each other; I pulled out the
envelope that was in the mailbox today and looked it over.  I studied it
carefully wondering if I had correctly guessed who it was from.

“What’s that?”  Nick asked.

“A letter from an old friend, I think.”  I looked at Nick and
caught Gabrielle’s stare, her eyes were trying to tell me something;
she was yelling into my head, hollering for me to leave.  She wanted
Nick to herself now and I had become a nuisance.  I wanted to be angry
but lately I’ve had my fill of it, I smiled lightly and left telling
Nick I would call him soon.

There was a note from Gale tacked to the refrigerator that said she was
going to spend the night with one of her newspaper lovers—a rock sat
in the pit of my stomach.  I had hoped that, with all my recent
revelations, I could tell my story to flesh and blood, but, as usual, it
was too much to ask.  Frequently in such times, I turned to the
typewriter, it was the only real friend I had and I didn’t mind so
much that it was inanimate, lately, I find writing therapeutic.  Soon I
would have no use for Gale and that would be a wondrous day when she
discovered it.

She liked to complain that I spent too much time alone, I wasn’t
trying to fulfill the debauched life we had agreed on.  I told her I
needed to settle first, get comfortable, and then I would work on it.

were really like between us.

Things were starting to come back into my life, after the fierce rains,
the sun had shone itself down upon me.  I felt good, nostalgic, and
almost vibrant.  The day beautiful, I am free to think and believe in
things I want.  It’s early, I have a cigarette on the deck and absorb
the sun; Gale didn’t come back during the night and I’m glad she
hadn’t, I can now enjoy the day.  I don’t want her hanging over me,
complaining that the place isn’t clean enough or that she works harder

friend was trying to track me down for what reason I know not; people of
long ago were trying to find me—friends and lovers—wanting me to
spend time with them.  Perhaps they sensed the turmoil I had recently
gone through, felt my anger and resentment, and knew the changes I had
commissioned.  But then again I really had no idea.

The evening at Nick’s left me pensive, disillusioned, and a little
pissed.  I went out for a walk, the one I wanted to take

almost immaculately clean streets; the planted trees showed their green,
the flowers neatly tended, the grass in the park painstakingly mowed. 
There was one bum and many snot-nosed pseudo-intellectual rich people. 
They must have come out of the hills, the streets were littered with

hearing on the radio Walnut Creek has banned smoking anywhere in town. 
I suppose when you get your big money, you get self-righteous and start

dollars in my pocket, was only interested in getting a haircut and
didn’t give a damn what others did.  I only found fantastic perm
shops, gave up on getting a haircut, and settled in the park near the
post office.  A man was sitting alone reading the paper and smoking; I
felt at home, no frosted albatross would glare at me here.

I extracted the letter and held it in my hand.  I turned in over to try
to catch a glimpse of the writing inside—the address was typed. 
Giving up on trying to guess, I ripped the envelope open.  The writing
was very familiar, it was from Jon Tyler, a friend from the East days
nearly six years ago.  I figured he must have something important to
tell me.

“Chris:

Long time, no hear.  Finally tracked you down, got your address from
your parents.  What have you been doing?  I’ve been good.  I’m in
California now, in San Rafael.  Give me a call when you can, let’s get
together.”

Jon

415-737-0137

The letter was brief, he hastily signed his name and scribbled the
phone number.  I lit up a smoke.  This was strange, my friend from years
ago tracking me down; I felt weird since he didn’t give a reason for
writing me.  We’ve been trying to disappear from each other for such a
long time now, it had been a competition for a while—why this sudden
communication?  I sighed heavily, he broke the deal.  There was this
challenge between us, ever since we were teenagers, to see who could do
the better job of vanishing.  It was easy to find him, he had always
lived with his parents; I had moved to Rhode Island, then to California,
hid in San Francisco, and now was in San Mateo—it seemed that I had
won.  But he had found me.  I took a long drag and held the smoke in, I
released it slowly and looked around me.  It was still sunny although a
few clouds were rolling in.  I wished I had some paper with me, my best
thoughts always occurred when I was away from the typewriter and without
a pen a paper.  Thinking of the past and how easy it was in youth—I
was healthy, ignorant, and melancholy.  I hoped I had gotten over most
of the misanthropy by channeling it into writing but I couldn’t forget
the people I had come to know.

She’s standing before me wrapped in a bed sheet; it’s dark and
she’s chastising me for being so immature and weak.  I smile
gloatingly at her, impervious to her insults.

“You always try to make me pity you, you’re so sickly, you don’t
deserve anything out of life, not the least bit of success.”  She
flutters the sheet around her, she’s burning up, near the point of
combustion from the heat of anger and resentment.  She will explode at
any moment.  Around her I see the universe, far off worlds where I have
been; trees, field grasses, rusted automobiles, and endless blue skies
reaching beyond the horizon.  I wonder if she has anything good to say
about me, if I write well or was a hack—she’ll probably invest more
in the hack because it was more convenient.  It matters not.  This woman
wrapped in cloth, she’s not at all like the woman I dream about; Anna
can put up with me, likes my company, is crazy and compliments me
occasionally so I won’t fall into despair—she cares.  But what do I
know?  Perhaps I’m just an irresponsible writer listening to the wrong
people and refusing to feel good about myself, hmm. . .that’s what she
likes to think, she wants me to be weak so she can continue to degrade
me.  Sometimes I find I act that way because I’m lazy and don’t
really care; yes, she’s so damn mature and like a rock, she needs no
one this woman wrapped in a sheet—not even me.  She has no heart, she
extracted it long ago; one day she may grow a new one and start to feel,
but for now she won’t.  One thing though, I promised myself that I
wouldn’t end up like her hating everything around me, doing
exclusively what I wanted and expecting everybody to be like me.  I
wanted to tell her, scream at her that the whole scenario was a bust and
there was no way I would be like her—if only I could find the proper
words.

stuff at home and save the money.  There I had a selection of ravioli

sounded good but the day was so nice, so warm, there wouldn’t be many
more afternoons like this feeling no pressures or responsibilities, not
having to please anybody—just live.  On days like this when I feel
good, I like to think about earlier times and the future, how I
wouldn’t mind saving up some money and moving back to the East coast
just for the hell of it.  To get the memories out of my system and
absolve my being from the tragedies of life.

I talked myself into having a late breakfast in a small cafe wedged in

dressed woman in a business skirt and jacket was sitting at the table

population is so helpless, they needed so much babying.  Their meager
lives needed to be controlled.

The woman in the suit looked at me a couple of times, the unlit cig was
dangling from my mouth; she was trying to figure me out—I was wearing
a tie and fordora.  She probably thought I was an office worker in town

and the cigarette. . .

She didn’t say anything to me, got up, and left.  I watched her

stupid seeing the main problem was how I would describe my physical and

when it came to the truth about me I couldn’t do it. . .I couldn’t
move my pen.

I went back to the apartment a little later and phoned Jon; I stopped
myself three times in the middle of dialing.  I didn’t know what to

“Huh, hello?”

“Jon, it’s Chris.”

“Oh, shit I’m sorry.  I didn’t recognize your voice.”

Yeah right.  If there was one thing I remembered clearly about Jon, it

He gave me some questionable directions.  “See you at seven.”

“Yeah, bye.”

Truth, I find, is stranger than fiction.  You could sit down with the
most creative crowd and ask them to brainstorm out a plot for a novel or
screenplay and not even come close to what you could find in everyday
life.  Characters derived wholly from your imagination would be bland
and the plot—you’d bore the reader to tears.  You pull from life,
from situations you see on the streets or read in the papers, exaggerate
just a little bit and bingo, you’ve got something there.  Maybe have
the setting even stranger putting it in the most colorful part of town
or country, and you’ve got a Pulitzer Prize winner beneath your
fingers.  It’s hiding within the recesses of the pen or typewriter.

preferred pure experience without default.

dreamt.

don’t feel the heat.  A couple of cars pass on the road, and up on the

I hear the door slam at the house.  A man came out to greet me, a large

300 pounds.  He walked confidently.

“Yeah?”

I nodded.  “This car’s for sale?”

He looked to the car then to me.  “You gonna buy it?  It’s a ‘48,
one of the best years for Packard.  Nice, huh?”

“A little beat up. . .”

He put his hands in his pockets.  “What makes you say that?”

“Well, the paint’s faded and the roof. . .” his expression turned
harsh.  “. . .and, well. . .”

“What do you expect?  It’s pretty fucking old.”  He smiled. 
“But she runs like a top, I tell you.”

He pulled the keys out of his pocket and got behind the wheel, I
climbed in the other side pushing fabric from the roof out of my face.

cranked but didn’t catch.

hesitantly.

I slid to the driver’s seat and put my hands on the wheel.  He shut
the door.

“Anything you think of. . .” I watched him through the rear view
mirror as he went back into the house.   As the screen door batted up
against its frame, the wind became listless.  Laughter roared from the
house, I put the Packard into gear and turned onto the road.

The dry air felt good, I flipped a switch and put all the windows down
and let the wind rip through the car.

Chapter 9	

It was nearly three weeks into October and I found myself, for the
first time, worrying how I was going to pay the rent.  Gale insisted I
needn’t concern myself about it, since we were going to be moving out
at the end of the month.  But I wasn’t too keen on the idea of
following her around, living in one place or another with her in all her
insane bitchiness—playing the role of her little playmate and resident
target for abuse.  Right now I’m not in the mood for worrying.  Things
seemed to be okay, maybe getting better; my job was becoming more
bearable, and I had faith in the future.  I wondered if things were
turning, that I was finally out of the woods. . .so to speak.

The day was bright and the air was clean, a brisk breeze shook the
trees; the scent of eucalyptus hung in the air.  I breathed deeply as I
walked to my car.  It fired right up, and I put down the window.  The
traffic was light and for the first time in months I didn’t purposely
slam the car door as I walked into work.  Now, I realize I hadn’t been
at this job for very long, maybe six months or so, but I knew that there
was never reason to worry about losing it.  I could not show up or
forget to wear the proper uniform (disgusting navy blue pants, oil
soaked boots and a matching blue shirt with my name tackily embroidered
across the breast), and they would still love me just the same.  It was
a sight to say the least.  I kept trying to convince myself that this
place was only temporary, a weigh-station, and soon I would have some
sort of success where I wouldn’t require a job, and move on.  I tried
to hold on dearly to that way of thinking; the people I worked with only
enforced the need to achieve something better.  I would be asked
continually:

“How long are you planning to stay?”

And I would reply with a simple,

“I don’t know.”  

And that was the truth.

“Okay, so you don’t know,” a co-worker, Steve, would say. 
“What the hell do you want to do?”

I smile broadly.  “Well, I’ve always thought of myself as a
writer.”

Steve smirked.  “A writer, huh?  Working on anything?”

And that was the snag.  I could tell him anything I wanted.  I could
say, ‘Well, I’m working on my second novel’, or stretch the truth
a little bit, ‘I’m finishing a short story now, and think it will be
published soon’.  Or perhaps that was an out and out lie.  The problem
was I thought of myself as a writer before I actually achieved it; but
then again the central drive for an artist comes from within and if you
have faith, then success is an unavoidable consequence.  And I had
plenty of faith, a whole fucking truckload of it.

On the other hand, maybe I should tell these people the truth about my
situation.  Yeah right.  Tell these unappreciative clods the truth, bear
out all my insecurities so in their simple minds they’ll have
something to give me shit about.  Scenarios would unfold like:

“Oh, yes, Chris wants to be a writer (mockingly); he’s just so
intelligent.  Oh, Gale, you prophetic guru, let’s go to the library
and talk about literature”. . .yeah, I need more of this shit in my
life.  I need these loser assholes, doomed to a meager existence,
telling me what I should or should not do.  And although Steve really
hadn’t said anything, I found myself hating him.  I was sick at the
sight of his gaunt face.  In an instant I could have smashed all his
teeth out with the iron bar I had in my hand, but I figured that would
be a little too dramatic.  I opted for a better approach.  I lied to
him.

I may say that I like my job, that it pays the bills; or that  I like
cars, or that I am more financially secure, but it’s bullshit.  In
reality, I hate everything about it.  Sometimes, though, I find that
through all this hate, a glimpse of understanding emerges.  Yeah, the
job is simplistic and dirty, but I do get to be alone most of the time
and am allowed to think of things other than what I’m doing.  Things
like, what does life hold for me?  What do I hope to achieve?  What in
God’s name am I doing?!  Thoughts deep; so deep sometimes that I find
myself dying to get away from them.  Escapism is a simple art, one does
not necessarily need to do the mainstream method like driving long
distances, stealing money from the estranged wife or girlfriend and
hopping a plane to France, or even walking away.  The true artist can
merely explore the recesses of his mind, find himself in different
situations, and try out different lifestyles.  For me, I find the
companionship of others a release. . .but only under the right set of
circumstances.  I feel the need to control the situation, be at the
helm, the captain of the ship giving orders to the others, knowing
they’re responding to me the way I desire them to.  They must obey my
every whim.  But these days, I find the ship has gone, the others have
deserted me.  I’m nearly alone and moving toward complete desolation. 
So, at times, escape has its drawbacks, but that transcendence toward
emptiness is a road I cannot veer from.  I know I set the course in
youth, and through my stubbornness am destined to that end.  I didn’t
know when, but soon I would disappear from this life, and perhaps that
was the crux of feeling little care for the job (not that I loved it in
the first place).  If they fired me, I couldn’t care less.  If Gale
threw me out on my ass, I didn’t give a shit.  If someone shot me or
else I committed suicide, I wouldn’t give a damn.  But all this was
moot, the weight of life’s drastic changes rarely fell on my
shoulders.  Anything that happened did so on my own accord, and the
residual negativity was for others to bear.

The car I was working on had been frustrating me for some time.  My
thoughts were not so playful as they had been at the start.  I was
becoming increasingly irritated.  Grease was caked to my hands, and
smeared across my face; dirt hung loosely in my hair which dangled
before my eyes and poked them.  A frozen bolt pushed me near the
breaking point, I was swearing and fighting the damn thing.  And the
inevitable happens:  the wrench slipped and my hand went into a twisted
mass of metal that, of course, had many sharp edges.  My hand ached, the
blood ran mixed with the grease and I yelled.  The wrench went flying
across the shop and landed in a loud series of clangs.

I had the chance to take a break later in the day.  I lay down on a
vinyl couch in the basement, and closed my eyes for a few moments.  My
hand throbbed, the pulsing pain a metronome; I let my mind relax and
wander.  I couldn’t wait to be out of work, driving away, but then
again I had to face Gale and I hoped she wouldn’t be home tonight. 
Maybe she had already gone out with one of her newspaper

I remember arguments with her about Bruce’s “art”.

“He’s a writer, Chris, just like you.”

“Not a writer and most certainly NOT like me.”

“C’mon, me writes, he’s had a few books published.  He’s better
off than you.”

“I don’t think travelogues count.”

“Well, he’s more successful than you.”

Now it was starting to hurt.  “How old is he?  Late thirties?  So
what?  He’s got a small dick and comes in thirty seconds.  He’s not

“That’s what I like about him.  He gives me all this free

REWORK:  Time for my mind to wander onto something else.  It was
Thursday, I had to work tomorrow and had the weekend off, what was I
going to do?  Anything?  I could go driving out of town. . .any parties.
. .yes!  I sprung from my seat and thrust my eyes open.  The party at

But my hopes were dashed as soon as I arrived at the apartment.

“Somebody called for you, the number’s on the desk.”

The sound of the television broke the stillness of the early evening. 
She was yelling the answers to Jeopardy as I picked up the torn, hastily
scribbled upon piece of paper.  I made out the name Theresa and a
number.

“Theresa called?”

“Yeah, about an hour ago.  Who’s that?”

I smiled to myself.  “Someone I used to fuck.”

“I don’t want to hear about it.”  

I picked up the phone and dialed the number. The line was busy.  I
tried again in a half-hour—busy.  And then a couple of hours later, no
answer.  I gave up on the idea.  Theresa had been a bitch to me the last

she couldn’t get into the debauched lifestyle.  She had no idea what
life was about or how to function in it.  No sense in trying to teach
her, she was unable to learn.

I let Friday pass without incident.  Gale came by telling me there was
a gathering at Colin’s house.  A grand old pseudo black magic

“Probably have a chance to get laid.”

Where’s the challenge, the spark of something new?”

“Well, there’ll be some other girls there, friends.  All you have

A guy from work walked by Gale’s car.  He noticed me and waved.

“Great.”

“No, I have something else to do.”

“What?”

“I’m going to Alameda to a party.”

“Oh, I see, hmm.  What time are you off?”

I looked at my watch.  “In about forty-five minutes or so.”

“Let’s have dinner tonight.  I’ll go over to the mall and wait
until you get out.”

Gale was plotting something, she was never this nice to me.  Or maybe
it was something else.

“Okay.”

“I think we should talk.”

“Sounds fine, but I have other things to do.”

“It won’t take long.  I promise.”

Although I still felt trepidation about meeting Gale, I found myself

sick.  I had, by charm and half-truth and fatigue, secured the means of
escape.  Yet driving back into the city, I did not feel free.  I felt
like a thief with a full tank.

May 21.  Gale called twice yesterday and three times today.  I let the
machine get it each time.  On the last message she laughed and said she
knew I was home and should stop acting like such a prima donna.  She
said Billy had tried calling again and she nearly threw up just hearing
his voice.  Then she said she missed me, then she said she hated me, and
then the tape clicked off.

I did not call her back.

Rhindy and I have become eerily civil.  This is worse in some ways than
all the fighting.  She has begun speaking to me as though I were already
gone.  She asks if I need a box.  She asks if I’m taking the lamp.  She
asks if the books by the bed are mine or hers.  At night we move around
each other carefully, like tenants who have confused a lease with love,
or maybe like a pair of actors after the show is over, still in costume
but too tired to take it off.

We still had sex one final time.  I suppose I should record it because
it happened, although it meant nothing more than inertia.  She came to
me after midnight in one of her old T-shirts, stood there silently until
I looked up, then sat beside me and put her hand on my thigh.  Neither
of us said anything.  It was tender only in the sense that a funeral can
be called graceful.  When it was over, she rolled away and pulled the
sheet up to her shoulder.

“Will you at least try to be decent to me this week?” she asked.

“I’ll try.”

“That’s not much.”

“It’s what I have.”

She began to cry then, very softly, which almost undid me.  Not enough
to stay, but enough to make me ashamed.

May 28.  I got the money together, every humiliating dollar of it.
What I had from work, what I borrowed, what I extracted from my parents,
what I kept in envelopes and under books and in jacket pockets.  I put
it all on the table and counted it twice.  It looked like a child’s
idea of a fortune.

Janet phoned in the evening and said everything was still set for the
first.  She sounded cheerful, almost excited, as if we were embarking on
some bright collegiate adventure and not simply rearranging the scenery
of another gradual collapse.

“You okay?” she asked.

“Sure.”

“You don’t sound sure.”

“I’m moving, Janet.  I don’t know what sure sounds like.”

She laughed and told me not to worry, that once I was out things would
be different.  I had heard that phrase from too many mouths lately.
Different.  Better.  Freer.  Cleaner.  As if geography could absolve the
soul.

Later that night I walked alone to the beach.  The air was cold and the
water black.  I tried to think of Heather and found I could only think
of the way she had said the thing that gets you out of there.  I tried
to think of Rhindy and
could only think of the apartment with my absence already built into it.
I tried to think of Ninety-Nine and for one fleeting second I saw her
again, not on the sand beside me but farther down the shoreline,
motionless, as if waiting to see whether I would finally understand
something.

I wanted to ask her if all this movement meant anything.  If leaving
counted as transformation.  If changing rooms and women and coastlines
and habits, or saying I would, counted as transformation.  If I had
done enough to justify the pain I had caused.  But she did not come
closer.  She only remained there in my mind, made of moonlight and
distance and some promise no human being had ever kept for very long.

June 1.  We moved the boxes in two trips.  It was overcast on the
peninsula, the kind of weather that makes every house seem temporary.
Janet was in good spirits, arranging books before the bed was even set
up, talking about curtains and coffee and where we might put the little
table by the window.  I brought in the last box and set it down in what
was now my room.  My room.  The phrase sounded theatrical, almost
embarrassing.

The place was quiet.  No Rhindy in the kitchen.  No notes on the table.
No expectation humming in the walls.  I stood in the center of the room
and waited for the rush of relief that had been promised by every nerve
in my body these last weeks.  It did not come.

What arrived instead was a vast and measured stillness.  I was out.  I
had done it.  I had extracted myself from one life and carried my books,
my typewriter, my clothes, my cigarettes, my bad habits, my fantasies,
my guilt, and my mind into another room in another town.  Yet there I
was, intact in all the ways I least wanted to be, still myself down to
the marrow, which was the one thing I had somehow hoped not to bring.

Janet knocked on the open door holding two cups of coffee.

“Well?” she said.  “How does freedom feel?”

I took the cup from her and looked around at the undecorated walls, the
window, the boxes still half-opened on the floor.

“Quiet,” I said.

“That’s all?”

“For now.”

She smiled and left me alone.  I set the coffee on the windowsill and
sat down at the typewriter.  Outside, the afternoon hung in a grey haze.
There was no ocean here, at least not in sight, but I could feel the old
pull of distance all the same.  Somewhere Rhindy was returning from work to an
apartment without me.  Somewhere Heather was living her life beyond the
weight of my projections.  Somewhere Gale was talking herself into or
out of another calamity.  And somewhere beyond all of them, beyond the
women and the rooms and the miles and the filth and the wanting, there
was still Ninety-Nine, not as a woman I could hold but as the shape of
everything I had failed to become.

I rolled a page into the machine and pressed my fingers to the keys.
The room was quiet enough to hear myself think, which is not always a
blessing.  I no longer had any excuses, or not the old ones anyway:
not Rhindy, not the apartment, not the noise of the city, not the
tyranny of needing to leave.  I had my freedom, such as it was.  And
the terrible thing about freedom is that, once granted, it expects an
answer.

I struck the first key.

For a long while I sat there listening to the single line I had made
and the silence around it.  Then I looked toward the window and in the
reflection, for the briefest moment, I thought I saw a woman with dark
red hair standing behind me, smiling not with promise but with knowing.
When I turned, there was no one there.

I faced the page again.

The only thing left to do was begin.
